  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_a570555b-9a3b-462c-9fb9-0cad538f4f7b'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

<div id="review_160746058" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/160746058" />
    <a title="Kemper" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/405390-kemper"><img alt="Kemper" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495476851p2/405390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/160746058?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kemper" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kemper" href="/user/show/405390-kemper">Kemper</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=2011">2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=serial-killers">serial-killers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=crime-mystery">crime-mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer160746058" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1609871105234711729">Nowadays, it seems like every horror movie is either a remake, a sequel or the kind of vile torture porn that makes you want to puke in your bag of popcorn. Filming one of these flicks requires tens of millions of dollars for a platoon of pretty actors, gallons of fake blood, special effects and a marketing campaign. Oddly, they don’t seem to spend any money on scripts for these things.<br/><br/>But Alfred Hitchcock only needed about nine grand to buy the rights to this book. Then it only took a blonde, a</span>
  <span id="freeText1609871105234711729" style="display:none">Nowadays, it seems like every horror movie is either a remake, a sequel or the kind of vile torture porn that makes you want to puke in your bag of popcorn. Filming one of these flicks requires tens of millions of dollars for a platoon of pretty actors, gallons of fake blood, special effects and a marketing campaign. Oddly, they don’t seem to spend any money on scripts for these things.<br /><br />But Alfred Hitchcock only needed about nine grand to buy the rights to this book. Then it only took a blonde, a shower, and a butcher knife to create one of the defining scenes in horror history. There might be a lesson in that story somewhere, Hollywood.<br /><br />Legend has it that Hitchcock had all the available copies of the book bought up after he obtained the rights so that he could keep the story secret for his version. If that isn’t true, it should be. I’ve often wished that I had a way to temporarily blank out my memory of certain stories so that I could read or see them for the first time all over again and be completely surprised. Unfortunately, alcoholic blackouts are extremely unreliable at this so I just have to try and imagine what it would have been like to read this book before the story became a classic. I bet it was a complete mind fuck for those poor bastards who did read it back in 1959.<br /><br />It holds up remarkably well despite knowing the story and it being over 50 years old. Shifting narration to the inner dialogues of different characters was very effective, especially with Norman himself. My only real complaint is that I wished it would have been the sister Lila and not Mary who took the infamous shower because Lila is a shrill nagging harpy that annoyed the hell out of me.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1609871105234711729" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_1974370576" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1974370576" />
    <a title="Dan Schwent" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/870755-dan-schwent"><img alt="Dan Schwent" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593973948p2/870755.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1974370576?book_show_action=true">Apr 17, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan Schwent" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan Schwent" href="/user/show/870755-dan-schwent">Dan Schwent</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/9647365-psycho">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=2017-books">2017-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=2017">2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1974370576" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10209588732065317680">When Mary Crane skips town with $40,000 of her boss's money, she drives and drives, bedding down at the Bates Motel. She meets Norman Bates, who harbors secrets even more interesting than stolen money...<br/><br/>Everyone knows the basic beats of Psycho due to the iconic Alfred Hitchcock film. Woman gets knifed in the shower, psychotic mama's boy, etc. When it popped up for ninety-nine cents, I figured, what the hell? <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2261903.Shooting_Star___Spiderweb' title='Shooting Star / Spiderweb by Robert Bloch' rel='nofollow noopener'>Shooting Star / Spiderweb</a> was pretty good. Psycho was definitely worth the buck.<br/><br/>Inspired</span>
  <span id="freeText10209588732065317680" style="display:none">When Mary Crane skips town with $40,000 of her boss's money, she drives and drives, bedding down at the Bates Motel. She meets Norman Bates, who harbors secrets even more interesting than stolen money...<br /><br />Everyone knows the basic beats of Psycho due to the iconic Alfred Hitchcock film. Woman gets knifed in the shower, psychotic mama's boy, etc. When it popped up for ninety-nine cents, I figured, what the hell? <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2261903.Shooting_Star___Spiderweb" title="Shooting Star / Spiderweb by Robert Bloch" rel="nofollow noopener">Shooting Star / Spiderweb</a> was pretty good. Psycho was definitely worth the buck.<br /><br />Inspired by real-life serial killer Ed Gein, Psycho is a tale of mental turmoil and the way it shapes the life a man dominated by his mother. And some woman gets killed and her boyfriend and sister try to figure out what the hell happened. Despite knowing quite a bit going in, Psycho was still a suspenseful read. Since stuff gets lost in translation from book to movie, a lot of it was still surprising. Of course, not having seen the movie in something like thirty years helped...<br /><br />Bloch's prose is pretty tight. He doesn't waste a lot of time on flowery language, and knows how to ratchet up the suspense. I can see why Hitchcock chose to adapt it, though he chose to focus on different aspects than Bloch. The book and the movie are definitely different animals.<br /><br />Psycho probably didn't have quite as much of an impact on me that it should have but that's because it's been dissected and imitated to death in the decades since it was written. It holds up really well compared to a lot of suspense novels written during the same era. Four out of five stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10209588732065317680" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6971034933887547366">Find all of my reviews at: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://52bookminimum.blogspot.com/'>http://52bookminimum.blogspot.com/</a><br/><br/>4.5 Stars<br/><br/><a href='http://www.freeimagehosting.net/commercial-photography/texas/houston/' rel='nofollow noopener'>

I hope they turn out just as friendly and loyal to their momma as Norman did.<br/><br/>Is there anyone even on the planet who hasn’t at least heard of <i>Psycho</i> before? What can I say that you don’t already know? Well, I can confirm that this book is <b>short</b> at roughly 200 pages. Due to its brevity, I can also say not a paragraph is wasted on filler. Every scene that occurs does so for a reason. W</span>
  <span id="freeText6971034933887547366" style="display:none">Find all of my reviews at: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://52bookminimum.blogspot.com/">http://52bookminimum.blogspot.com/</a><br><br>4.5 Stars<br><br><a href="http://www.freeimagehosting.net/commercial-photography/texas/houston/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1431960583ra/14887039.gif" alt="Houston commercial photography" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>That’s what I tell my boys <b>all</b> the time. I hope they turn out just as friendly and loyal to their momma as Norman did.<br><br>Is there anyone even on the planet who hasn’t at least heard of <i>Psycho</i> before? What can I say that you don’t already know? Well, I can confirm that this book is <b>short</b> at roughly 200 pages. Due to its brevity, I can also say not a paragraph is wasted on filler. Every scene that occurs does so for a reason. What else? Ah yes, the writing. If I hadn’t already been privy to the fact this was an oldie by a goodie, I would have never guess this story to be 50+ years old. The subject matter as well as use of graphic language is not something I’m accustomed to when reading books that are a bit long in the tooth. Bloch had <b>zero</b> fear when tackling the taboo, that’s for sure. If you’re someone with a bit of a . . . dark sense of humor like me, you might even find yourself getting a morbid chuckle or two . . . <br><br><b>
  <i>“No sense crying over spilt milk, though. Even if it hadn’t been milk.”</i>
</b><br><br>As is the case with me and most things dubbed “horror” I wouldn’t put <i>Psycho</i> in that category. Instead I’d call it a riveting page-turner of a suspense. One that I’d highly recommend to all.<br><br>If you’re not interested in the book, but rather the film I ask you – WHAT THE F*&amp;^ ARE YOU DOING ON A BOOKNERD SITE?!?!?!? Nah, just kidding. The differences in the translation from page to screen lie almost exclusively in the casting. As my friend <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1266267037?book_show_action=false&amp;page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Ed</a> called out in his review, the book version Norman Bates was a fella who was a bit festively plump – proving that the casting of this guy . . . <br><br><a href="http://www.freeimagehosting.net/commercial-photography/texas/houston/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1431960584ra/14887040.gif" alt="Houston commercial photography" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>was spot on if the remake had been done a few years later. Poor Vince. Remember when he was a real looker????<br><br><a href="http://www.freeimagehosting.net/commercial-photography/texas/houston/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1431960584ra/14887041.gif" alt="Houston commercial photography" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>(Well, not compared to Jeff Goldblum, but he wasn’t anything to chuck a rock at)<br><br>The only other Hitchcockian magic? Tweaking the following line into one of the most quotable of all times . . . <br><br><b>
  <i>“I think perhaps all of us go a little crazy at times.”</i>
</b><br><br>and creating another use for chocolate syrup ; )<br></span>

<div id="review_1677541810" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1677541810" />
    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1677541810?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1677541810" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17767718390564281274">We all go a little crazy sometimes.<br/><br/>My generation and everyone since has grown up with the concept of Psycho, stemming from Sir Alfred Hitchcock’s 1960 thriller, but all this began with Robert Bloch’s 1959 novel.<br/><br/>Reading this after having seen the film and grown up with the story, I dealt with a fair amount of theatrical irony. While the film stayed mostly true to Bloch’s vision, there were some departures and these were enjoyable to experience. <br/><br/>Bloch’s prose is tight and the atmosphere developed </span>
  <span id="freeText17767718390564281274" style="display:none">We all go a little crazy sometimes.<br><br>My generation and everyone since has grown up with the concept of Psycho, stemming from Sir Alfred Hitchcock’s 1960 thriller, but all this began with Robert Bloch’s 1959 novel.<br><br>Reading this after having seen the film and grown up with the story, I dealt with a fair amount of theatrical irony. While the film stayed mostly true to Bloch’s vision, there were some departures and these were enjoyable to experience. <br><br>Bloch’s prose is tight and the atmosphere developed is intense and suspenseful. I imagined the thrill of a reader in 1959 reading this narrative for the first time as it moved along in Bloch’s masterful recitation and how surprising and thrilling some of the realizations must have been. This is also a seminal work for the horror genre, both in literature and in film, and some of the techniques Bloch used to such great effect more than 50 years ago are still tried and true today.<br><br>Turns out the apple did not fall far from the tree. Robert Bloch was heavily influenced by, and in fact mentored by, none other than H.P. Lovecraft himself. The young Bloch was encouraged by the older writer and Lovecraft even dedicated a story to Bloch, “The Haunter of the Dark” (1936). Both writers were frequent contributors to Weird Tales and Amazing Stories. Bloch’s earliest writing was unapologetically influenced by Lovecraft and was even frequently set in the Cthulhu mythos.<br><br>Still creepy and psychologically terrifying decades later, a fan of this genre will want to discover the origin of Norman Bates and will be enthralled by Bloch’s excellent work.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1466867060i/19527609.jpg" width="275" height="151" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="17767718390564281274" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2874692127" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2874692127" />
    <a title="Sheri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/63328133-sheri"><img alt="Sheri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483670555p2/63328133.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2874692127?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sheri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sheri" href="/user/show/63328133-sheri">Sheri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590203354" href="/book/show/7894085-psycho">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63328133-sheri?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63328133-sheri?shelf=2019">2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2874692127" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6805877407209557600">I’ve always shied away from books in the horror genre as I think they will be too gruesome for me. The violence, the blood, guts, and gore, and overall creepiness are not things I enjoy reading about or envisioning in any way. I decided to give Psycho a chance as it’s a classic horror read. I didn’t know much going in – just that the main character is Norman Bates, he lives at a hotel with his mother, and there is both a TV series and a movie based on the book. I figured if things got too appall</span>
  <span id="freeText6805877407209557600" style="display:none">I’ve always shied away from books in the horror genre as I think they will be too gruesome for me. The violence, the blood, guts, and gore, and overall creepiness are not things I enjoy reading about or envisioning in any way. I decided to give Psycho a chance as it’s a classic horror read. I didn’t know much going in – just that the main character is Norman Bates, he lives at a hotel with his mother, and there is both a TV series and a movie based on the book. I figured if things got too appalling and repulsive I could always quit reading.<br /><br />So with trepidation, I began reading and found myself quite surprised at how tame this novel is. The acts of violence that I was so worried about are succinctly mentioned but not described in any great detail. The story itself was quite short and rather slow at times and in the end, I must say I found it rather anticlimactic. I thought there would be more – more ghastliness, more psychological illumination, just plain more than this relatively simple story. The world is a different place from when this book was first published 60 years ago, and perhaps I am more accustomed to the awful atrocities that happen both in plain sight and behind the scenes, and thus the shock value wasn’t there for me. But even considering that, I still found this to be undramatic and lacking the punch that would elevate it to a truly suspenseful and horrifying read.<br /><br />Many reviewers said that this is a case where the movie is better than the book, so I’ll be watching the movie soon to see if I like it better. I am glad I gave the book a chance, and it will be interesting to see how it compares to the movie.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6805877407209557600" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText13749202811758154451" style="display:none">First published in 1959, there is no doubt about it, <b>PSYCHO</b> is an absolutely <b>great</b> horror classic.<p>If by some freak of nature you happen to be in the dark regarding Robert Bloch's <b>Psycho</b> I will warn you <b>not</b> to go in the shower at the Bates Motel, and be green with envy that you can read the novel with no prior knowledge of the intriguing plot.</p><p>If you <b>are</b> familiar with Alfred Hitchcock's movie version <i>(released in 1960)</i> then you will notice two obvious differences when reading the book, the first one <i>(no biggie)</i> is Norman's rather large size, and the shocker, well, the shower scene is actually a bit more grotesque <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[resulting in a bloody severed head. Janet Leigh's pretty head is still attached in the movie version with a super close-up of her <b>DEAD EYE</b> staring right at <b>YOU.</b> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></p><p>Overall, I actually liked the movie better than the book, and was all set to give it 4 Stars, but decided that was unfair for if I had not been familiar with the plot from seeing the movie numerous times in my youth, I would surely have given a rating of 5 Big Ones......despite all the typos.</p></span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1438274799?book_show_action=true">Nov 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Idarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Idarah" href="/user/show/1283951-idarah">Idarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/22891277-psycho">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1283951-idarah?shelf=read-2015">read-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1438274799" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7644436412389991364"><b>“We're all not quite as sane as we pretend to be.”</b><br/><br/>I am a great fan of Alfred Hitchcock and his films, but this is the only film that I haven't watched more than once. Sure, that includes a string of other Oscar winners like<i> Shawshank Redemption </i>and <i>Forest Gump; </i>I'm just a weirdo. Psycho really scared me when I was young, though. There was no way to explain how the silhouette of mother's chair rocked on its own while Norman was at the hotel. I don't like unexplainable things.<br/><br/>The novel was actuall</span>
  <span id="freeText7644436412389991364" style="display:none"><b>“We're all not quite as sane as we pretend to be.”</b><br /><br />I am a great fan of Alfred Hitchcock and his films, but this is the only film that I haven't watched more than once. Sure, that includes a string of other Oscar winners like<i> Shawshank Redemption </i>and <i>Forest Gump; </i>I'm just a weirdo. Psycho really scared me when I was young, though. There was no way to explain how the silhouette of mother's chair rocked on its own while Norman was at the hotel. I don't like unexplainable things.<br /><br />The novel was actually really good! It was very psychological and Bloch gave the reader a copilot seat into Norman's mind. It's not until the very end of the novel that we finally learn everything there is to know about Norman, his complex relationship with his deceased mother, and his questionable choice of reading material.<br /><br />For a novel written in 1959, this must have been pretty out there, but it was based on murderer Ed Gein, who was arrested in 1957. He had murdered two woman and apparently exhumed grave sites regularly to showcase body parts. He was even in the process of creating a woman's skin suit to resemble his mother so that he could literally "crawl into her skin." That is some pretty twisted stuff. It makes this book and any other Gein inspired characters appear mild (nod to Buffalo Bill). And now for a break in reading material. Phew!</span>
  <a data-text-id="7644436412389991364" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

In the end out of guilt he tried to revive his mother from dead having conversation with her skeleton and pretending she was alive until in his split personality she literally became part of him. He would become semi drunk and have and split where she became the driver and killed until he gained control again, then he would assume he just passed out asleep when she was in control.         rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12656534-zoeytron?shelf=public-library">public-library</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1270732690" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5676028954178871553">Horror is my "go to" genre, my bread and butter. Although more than 50 years have passed since the movie was made, Psycho remains at the top of the tree on my list of best horror flicks. It's dated, certainly. Filmed in black and white, complete with melodramatic music and exaggerated close-ups, but it works beautifully by leaving something to the imagination. <br/><br/>The book, written in 1959, stands the proverbial test of time, as well. 'You do not want Mother using her keys.' Quite right. The poundin</span>
  <span id="freeText5676028954178871553" style="display:none">Horror is my "go to" genre, my bread and butter. Although more than 50 years have passed since the movie was made, Psycho remains at the top of the tree on my list of best horror flicks. It's dated, certainly. Filmed in black and white, complete with melodramatic music and exaggerated close-ups, but it works beautifully by leaving something to the imagination. <br /><br />The book, written in 1959, stands the proverbial test of time, as well. 'You do not want Mother using her keys.' Quite right. The pounding comes, then the roaring, followed by blessed silence. For a little while, at least. What a trip it would have been to have read the book before seeing the movie. But even with that ship having sailed decades ago, reading the novel was pure pleasure.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1266267037" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1266267037" />
    <a title="Edward Lorn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8850418-edward-lorn"><img alt="Edward Lorn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1492199506p2/8850418.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1266267037?book_show_action=true">Apr 28, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward Lorn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward Lorn" href="/user/show/8850418-edward-lorn">Edward Lorn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/25443365-psycho">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">horror and thriller fans</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1266267037" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12467897674222063101">Other than Norman being a Tubby Trooper in the book instead of the Starved Stanchion he is in the movie and television series, I found no glaring differences between the Hitchcock film and the book. I can't even say that the book is better than the movie. They are completely equal in my eyes. Now the sequels? I haven't a clue. I have not seen the later movies, nor have I read the follow-up novels Bloch published (<i>Psycho 2</i> and <i>Psycho House</i>). Now that I've tackled the first book, I feel comfortabl</span>
  <span id="freeText12467897674222063101" style="display:none">Other than Norman being a Tubby Trooper in the book instead of the Starved Stanchion he is in the movie and television series, I found no glaring differences between the Hitchcock film and the book. I can't even say that the book is better than the movie. They are completely equal in my eyes. Now the sequels? I haven't a clue. I have not seen the later movies, nor have I read the follow-up novels Bloch published (<i>Psycho 2</i> and <i>Psycho House</i>). Now that I've tackled the first book, I feel comfortable moving on. Just wanted to make sure there weren't any differences. <br /><br />Paul Michael Garcia does a terrific job with the Blackstone Audiobook. I don't know if there are other versions out there, but I enjoyed PMG's performance. This one comes in at 5:22 hours, so it's a little less than 3 hours if you bump up the speed. Worth a try. <br /><br />In summation: A classic. The big reveal gave me chills even though I knew it was coming. Recommended whether you've seen the movie or not.<br /><br /><b>Final Judgment: A perfect argument for poor hygiene.</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="12467897674222063101" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText2677834665572115383" style="display:none">First, it wasn't a scary as I thought it would be (which is a very good thing for me, if you're wondering) and secondly, I had foreseen everything that was going to happen at page 10 (which, to be honest, is not as good). So I blame it all to the passing of time and its effects, such as the immense fame it brought to this story.<br />I still didn't really enjoy it.</span>

  <span id="freeText7571914630275976014" style="display:none">The 1950's a simpler more innocent time when a man could dress up as his dead mother and kill people. Oh memories. Unless you live under a rock you know what Psycho is all about. The famous shower scene. I've seen the movie probably 40x I'm a Hitchcock fanatic but I had never read the book upon which the movie was based. I had watched a documentary about the man who inspired it Ed Gein, btw he was really psycho. I mean Mr. Gein inspired both Psycho and Buffalo Bill from Silence of the Lambs. Quite the gruesome accomplishment. The movie follows the book almost exactly. With one big change the Norman Bates of the book is in his 40's and overweight. I personally preferred the movie version &amp; while reading I thought of Anthony Perkins the entire time. Who would I recommend Psycho to Hitchcock fans obviously &amp; readers who like dark &amp; twisted stories.</span>

<div id="review_492299233" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/492299233" />
    <a title="Susanne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1029787-susanne"><img alt="Susanne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1449517639p2/1029787.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/492299233?book_show_action=true">Jan 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susanne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susanne" href="/user/show/1029787-susanne">Susanne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer492299233" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5425832625782950342">What strikes me most about this book is: the things for which its movie is known are ABSENT from these pages.<br/><br/>The movie <i>Psycho</i> gave us that bedrock upon which all future slasher films were built - sex = death.<br/>The 'bad girl' - bad because she had premarital sex - will die. The virgin will live. (If you don't know these rules, you need to watch <i>Scream</i>.)<br/><br/><i>Psycho</i> the film actually <b>opens</b> in the bedroom, where our heroine is in her bra having just had sex with a man to whom she's not married.<br/><br/>Norman Bates</span>
  <span id="freeText5425832625782950342" style="display:none">What strikes me most about this book is: the things for which its movie is known are ABSENT from these pages.<br /><br />The movie <i>Psycho</i> gave us that bedrock upon which all future slasher films were built - sex = death.<br />The 'bad girl' - bad because she had premarital sex - will die. The virgin will live. (If you don't know these rules, you need to watch <i>Scream</i>.)<br /><br /><i>Psycho</i> the film actually <b>opens</b> in the bedroom, where our heroine is in her bra having just had sex with a man to whom she's not married.<br /><br />Norman Bates never sees any of this, by the way. He operates under his own set of rules. But the <i>viewer</i> knows what all she's done.<br /><br />In the book - and this isn't important in the book, so I don't think it's a spoiler - our heroine has <b>NOT</b> slept with her boyfriend. Wait for that to sink in.<br /><br />The whole kill-women-who-enjoy-their-sexuality thing is <b>NOT</b> in this book.<br /><br />Norman is still operating under his set of rules, but the VIEWER'S opinion of the girl doesn't matter. <br /><br />In fact, the reader is treated to a very sympathetic look inside our heroine's brain. She's a nice girl who sacrificed her whole life for her family, is put in a difficult situation, and ends up in the wrong place at the wrong time.<br /><br />There isn't much gore in this book either. <br /><br />The movie may have ushered in "violence-porn" as a category, but the book is more about edge-of-your-seat thrills than blood.<br /><br />One last note - the most competent character, when it comes to solving the crime, is a woman. <br /><br />I am forced to wonder what the film world would be like if the movie had stuck to the book in these respects.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5425832625782950342" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17161646635027737567"><b>4.5</b> I really enjoy the read. <b>Something about the writing is so very…well, psycho.</b> I’m sure, even if you know the big plot twist <i>–who doesn’t-</i> the book is addictive and completely interesting. It has a great inside-killer POV and a perfect pace,<b> the last chapter is pure psychological gold</b>. Special for psycho-thrillers fans and I would suggest reading the novel if you like the movie; you´ll definitely pay more attention to certain details. At the end Psycho is a great thriller book, it might have </span>
  <span id="freeText17161646635027737567" style="display:none"><b>4.5</b> I really enjoy the read. <b>Something about the writing is so very…well, psycho.</b> I’m sure, even if you know the big plot twist <i>–who doesn’t-</i> the book is addictive and completely interesting. It has a great inside-killer POV and a perfect pace,<b> the last chapter is pure psychological gold</b>. Special for psycho-thrillers fans and I would suggest reading the novel if you like the movie; you´ll definitely pay more attention to certain details. At the end Psycho is a great thriller book, it might have become the great hit thanks to its adaptation, but <b>Hitchcock respected the source material like a king. </b><br /><br /><br />Todo lo que se tiene que saber de forma general sobre Psicosis, yo ya lo sabía. Aun sin haber visto la película. De todas formas el libro me sorprendió mucho, tiene unos puntos de vista muy intensos e interesantes, sobretodo el de <b>Norman Bates, loco entre los locos!</b><br /><br />Si entran al libro creyendo ver más profundidad de la que se ve en la película, o detalles extras, se van a llevar una desilusión, la adaptación es increíblemente fiel <i>(y eso que no he visto la película entera, solo pedazos o lo que me han contado)</i> y creo que cualquier fan del genero estará encantado de leer Psicosis, definitivamente un <u>must</u> para nosotros los thriller-lovers(?), no solo por ser la obra que origino la película sino porque<b> la mente de Norman es un monumento a la psicología retorcida de personajes.</b> Un gran trabajo. <br /><br />Trama ágil y sin sobre enredos, y un personaje como pocos. <b>Altamente recomendado si buscas psicología fuerte pero también suspense del liviano y bueno.</b> Psicosis cumplirá a la perfección.</span>

<div id="review_1051161773" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1051161773" />
    <a title="Quentin Wallace" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33274222-quentin-wallace"><img alt="Quentin Wallace" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595984858p2/33274222.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1051161773?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Quentin Wallace" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Quentin Wallace" href="/user/show/33274222-quentin-wallace">Quentin Wallace</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1051161773" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16530747225847714892">If you ask the average person who wrote Psycho, they will probably say Alfred Hitchcock. But we here at Goodreads know better. The book and movie were released very close together, and the subject matter was really strong for the time. <br/><br/>I think the book and movie are probably equally as good, but the movie really went on to greater acclaim due to the direction of Hitchcock. The story was loosely based on Ed Gein (as was The Texas Chainsaw Massacre and a few more stories as well.) I think almost a</span>
  <span id="freeText16530747225847714892" style="display:none">If you ask the average person who wrote Psycho, they will probably say Alfred Hitchcock. But we here at Goodreads know better. The book and movie were released very close together, and the subject matter was really strong for the time. <br /><br />I think the book and movie are probably equally as good, but the movie really went on to greater acclaim due to the direction of Hitchcock. The story was loosely based on Ed Gein (as was The Texas Chainsaw Massacre and a few more stories as well.) I think almost anyone who saw the movie after its original release (or read the book after the movie as well) already knew the twist ending, but if you take a second and think about reading this book without knowing the ending, it takes the book to a whole...'nother...level.(I originally gave the book 4 stars, but that was because I knew the story before I read it. When you add in the surprise ending, it does add a star.)<br /><br />Robert Bloch is writer who both fit into the grouping of the Weird authors such as Lovecraft, but his career was lengthy enough he could also be grouped with more modern authors such as Stephen King. There aren't many that can fit into both groups, so I always thought this made him unique.<br /><br />I won't go any deeper into the storyline since I think we either all know it by now, but I would recommend this book to any fan of horror or suspense. Even if you have seen the movie, read the book. It's not a lengthy book and can be read in one or two sittings, so if this is one you've had on your reading list, I say go ahead and read it, you won't be disappointed!</span>
  <a data-text-id="16530747225847714892" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2140353658" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2140353658" />
    <a title="Vanessa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4636758-vanessa"><img alt="Vanessa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596200358p2/4636758.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2140353658?book_show_action=true">Oct 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vanessa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vanessa" href="/user/show/4636758-vanessa">Vanessa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780719810817" href="/book/show/17244074-psycho">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2140353658" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2263988198412675235"><i>Psycho</i> felt like the perfect autumnal Halloween-month read. I'm a big fan of the Hitchcock film adaptation, and I always wanted to read this, so was excited to see it on display in my local library. <br/><br/>Bloch's original novel is a quick read, despite having a not so fast-paced plot, and really allows you to get into the head of not only Mary Crane, but of Norman Bates as well. Norman's chapters were absolutely fascinating, and I loved seeing the constant narrative through his mind twisting and turni</span>
  <span id="freeText2263988198412675235" style="display:none"><i>Psycho</i> felt like the perfect autumnal Halloween-month read. I'm a big fan of the Hitchcock film adaptation, and I always wanted to read this, so was excited to see it on display in my local library. <br /><br />Bloch's original novel is a quick read, despite having a not so fast-paced plot, and really allows you to get into the head of not only Mary Crane, but of Norman Bates as well. Norman's chapters were absolutely fascinating, and I loved seeing the constant narrative through his mind twisting and turning as events unfolded. <br /><br />I thoroughly enjoyed this read, despite the fact that I knew everything that was going to happen. At times I did catch myself wondering if I would have been able to work out certain plot elements from the story alone, with no prior knowledge, but honestly I think Bloch would have kept me hanging. He uses so much misdirection that even as I was following the plot and really analysing certain sections, I could still see how as an author he'd be able to whip the rug out from under a reader's feet.<br /><br />The story doesn't have all that much to it, but the writing always felt succinct enough that the plot kept moving along, even at its slow pace. I would really recommend this to people who did like the Hitchcock film, but also to anyone who is interested in the plot and wants a quick thriller/horror read for the month of October (or at any time of the year!). And those constant cliffhanger endings on most of the chapters... good stuff.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2263988198412675235" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_175344574" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/175344574" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/175344574?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      501 Must Read Books (Mystery)
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=501">501</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=suspense-thriller">suspense-thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=with-movie">with-movie</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer175344574" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15303934002723181064">Well-written. Straightforward third-party alternating narrations. No wasted words. Memorable classic scenes that have the ability to stay in your mind for a long time. Who has not seen the movie? Who does not remember the shower scene? The house on the hill behind the hotel? The old woman sitting on a rocking chair by the window?<br/><br/>This is a classic crime book featuring the popular serial killer called <b>Norman Bates.</b> This 1959 book may not be the pioneer in this genre but the English film director a</span>
  <span id="freeText15303934002723181064" style="display:none">Well-written. Straightforward third-party alternating narrations. No wasted words. Memorable classic scenes that have the ability to stay in your mind for a long time. Who has not seen the movie? Who does not remember the shower scene? The house on the hill behind the hotel? The old woman sitting on a rocking chair by the window?<br /><br />This is a classic crime book featuring the popular serial killer called <b>Norman Bates.</b> This 1959 book may not be the pioneer in this genre but the English film director and producer Alfred Hitchcock (1899-1980) immortalized this via his 1960 film. I was born in 1964 but I only saw that popular movie adaptation a decade ago. I was a father then and already in my late 30's so it failed to scare me. <br /><br />Same as this book. I did not feel anything. I was hoping that the narrative and the dialogues would scare me just like the time that I finally read Thomas Harris' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23807.The_Silence_of_the_Lambs__Hannibal_Lecter__2_" title="The Silence of the Lambs (Hannibal Lecter #2) by Thomas Harris" rel="nofollow noopener">The Silence of the Lambs</a> (4 stars). I liked that 1991 Oscar-award winning movie but I thought I missed some of the dialogues because Anthony Hopkins had to lower his voice and make his lines sleazy to achieve the scary effect. So, when I was reading the book, that was the time that I finally appreciated the exact verbal tussles between Dr. Lectern and Clarisse so it was only then that I felt the depth of the situation and the psychological content of what they said to each other.<br /><br />That only-now appreciation was absent while reading this novel because Hitchcock's 1960 dialogues in the movie were spoken very clearly. Also, the movie was 100% faithful to the book, so reading the book really did not give me anything new.<br /><br />When I was still single and a young man, I used to like this kind of scary stories. However, when I became a father of a little girl who, like most of young kids, was afraid of the dark, I had to forget about my own ghosts just to show her that there was nothing to be afraid of when you happened to be in a dark room. Also, during that time that I still liked this kind of stories, I saw a lot of movies that were <i>Psycho</i> copy cats. Be it about a serial killer, a lunatic preserving dead people or somebody who has multiple personalities, I'd seen too many variants of those on the silver screen that I thought I was already immune when I reached the midpoint of my life.<br /><br />However, I do not regret buying and reading this book. It is still nice to know that the now-classic movie and many of its copycats came from this classic crime book. It is also nice to brag to my friends that I read the classic book instead of just saying that I watched that classic movie.<br /><br />Some of my friends here in Goodreads say that the book is better than the movie but there are more who say the other way around. For me, since the movie is 100% faithful to the book, they are both great.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15303934002723181064" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenny Baker" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenny Baker" href="/user/show/16404316-jenny-baker">Jenny Baker</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

I'm surprised this was a middle-of-the-road read for me. It's not bad, but I was expecting it to be a lot more exciting.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('yfYCX34cYOkQHmZA8hdX9WCxXk0F7UjWsUYP7MylcCORUdqZ50JQXloyANOXf/yamXKqS6OV6Fdxut5am2QPYw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('dF0yFGcaY37/wn8ljX97F8qLwQtz8JRlbkURPKYE78Ms+urS/kRTybXuGbboF9B4M0g1DdWINOSuucCK8cWQgw==')})
  }

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17720863135436917482">Robert Albert Bloch was a prolific American writer. He was the son of Raphael "Ray" Bloch (1884, Chicago-1952, Chicago), a bank cashier, and his wife Stella Loeb (1880, Attica, Indiana-1944, Milwaukee, WI), a social worker, both of German-Jewish descent.<br/><br/>Bloch wrote hundreds of short stories and over twenty novels, usually crime fiction, science fiction, and, perhaps most influentially, horror fict</span>
  <span id="freeText17720863135436917482" style="display:none">Robert Albert Bloch was a prolific American writer. He was the son of Raphael "Ray" Bloch (1884, Chicago-1952, Chicago), a bank cashier, and his wife Stella Loeb (1880, Attica, Indiana-1944, Milwaukee, WI), a social worker, both of German-Jewish descent.<br /><br />Bloch wrote hundreds of short stories and over twenty novels, usually crime fiction, science fiction, and, perhaps most influentially, horror fiction (<i>Psycho</i>). He was one of the youngest members of the Lovecraft Circle; Lovecraft was Bloch's mentor and one of the first to seriously encourage his talent.<br /><br />He was a contributor to pulp magazines such as <i>Weird Tales</i> in his early career, and was also a prolific screenwriter. He was the recipient of the Hugo Award (for his story "That Hell-Bound Train"), the Bram Stoker Award, and the World Fantasy Award. He served a term as president of the Mystery Writers of America.<br /><br />Robert Bloch was also a major contributor to science fiction fanzines and fandom in general. In the 1940s, he created the humorous character Lefty Feep in a story for <i>Fantastic Adventures</i>. He also worked for a time in local vaudeville, and tried to break into writing for nationally-known performers. He was a good friend of the science fiction writer Stanley G. Weinbaum. In the 1960's, he wrote 3 stories for <i>Star Trek</i>.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17720863135436917482" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
